<small><i>(October 2014)</i></small></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bp_world_energy_consumption_2016.gif" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/Bp_world_energy_consumption_2016.gif 2x" data-file-width="482" data-file-height="290" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bp_world_energy_consumption_2016.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe world\'s energy consumption (2015 data)<sup id="cite_ref-BP-Review-2013_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP-Review-2013-1">[1]</a></sup></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_Total_Primary_Energy_Consumption_by_Fuel_(2015).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/World_Total_Primary_Energy_Consumption_by_Fuel_%282015%29.svg/300px-World_Total_Primary_Energy_Consumption_by_Fuel_%282015%29.svg.png" width="300" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/World_Total_Primary_Energy_Consumption_by_Fuel_%282015%29.svg/450px-World_Total_Primary_Energy_Consumption_by_Fuel_%282015%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/World_Total_Primary_Energy_Consumption_by_Fuel_%282015%29.svg/600px-World_Total_Primary_Energy_Consumption_by_Fuel_%282015%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="898" data-file-height="431" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_Total_Primary_Energy_Consumption_by_Fuel_(2015).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nTotal primary consumption in the world, by fuel (2015).</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><b>World energy consumption</b> is the total <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> used by the entire <a href="/wiki/Human_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Human civilization">human civilization</a>. Typically measured per year, it involves all energy harnessed from every <a href="/wiki/Energy_source" class="mw-redirect" title="Energy source">energy source</a> applied towards humanity\'s endeavours across every single industrial and technological sector, across every country. It does not include energy from food, and the extent to which direct biomass burning has been accounted for is poorly documented. Being the power source metric of civilization, World Energy Consumption has deep implications for humanity\'s socio-economic-political sphere.</p>\n<p>Institutions such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> (IEA), the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a> (EIA), and the <a href="/wiki/European_Environment_Agency" title="European Environment Agency">European Environment Agency</a> record and publish energy data periodically. Improved data and understanding of World Energy Consumption may reveal systemic trends and patterns, which could help frame current energy issues and encourage movement towards collectively useful solutions.</p>\n<p>Closely related to energy consumption is the concept of total <a href="/wiki/Primary_energy" title="Primary energy">primary energy</a> supply (TPES), which - on a global level - is the sum of energy production minus storage changes. Since changes of energy storage over the year are minor, TPES values can be used as an estimator for energy consumption. However, TPES ignores conversion efficiency, overstating forms of energy with poor conversion efficiency (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, gas and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear</a>) and understating forms already accounted for in converted forms (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_system" title="Photovoltaic system">photovoltaic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>). The IEA estimates that, in 2013, total primary energy supply (TPES) was 1.575 \xc3\x97 10<sup>17</sup> <a href="/wiki/Watt_hour" class="mw-redirect" title="Watt hour">Wh</a> (= 157.5 PWh, 5.67 \xc3\x97 10<sup>20</sup> <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joules</a>, or 13,541 <a href="/wiki/Tonne_of_oil_equivalent" title="Tonne of oil equivalent">Mtoe</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-Report-keyworld-2015_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-Report-keyworld-2015-2">[2]</a></sup> From 2000\xe2\x80\x932012 coal was the source of energy with the largest growth. The use of oil and natural gas also had considerable growth, followed by hydropower and renewable energy. Renewable energy grew at a rate faster than any other time in history during this period. The demand for nuclear energy decreased, in part due to nuclear disasters (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island</a> 1979, <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl</a> 1986, and <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima</a> 2011).<sup id="cite_ref-BP-Review-2013_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP-Review-2013-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-undp_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-undp-3">[3]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2011, expenditures on energy totalled over 6 trillion USD, or about 10% of the world <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP). Europe spends close to one-quarter of the world\'s energy expenditures, North America close to 20%, and Japan 6%.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>\n<div />\n<small>(Mtoe 13,371)</small></div>\n</td>\n<td>\n<div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;line-height: 100%; padding: 4px 0 0;">104,426<br />\n<small>(Mtoe 8,979)</small></div>\n</td>\n<td>22,668</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-IEA-Report-keyworld-2014_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-Report-keyworld-2014-7">[7]</a></sup></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2013</td>\n<td>\n<div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;line-height: 100%; padding: 4px 0 0;">157,482<br />\n<small>(Mtoe 13,541)</small></div>\n</td>\n<td>\n<div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;line-height: 100%; padding: 4px 0 0;">108,171<br />\n<small>(Mtoe 9,301)</small></div>\n</td>\n<td>23,322</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2014</td>\n<td>\n<div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;line-height: 100%; padding: 4px 0 0;">155,481<br />\n<small>(Mtoe 13,369)</small></div>\n</td>\n<td>\n<div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;line-height: 100%; padding: 4px 0 0;">109,613<br />\n<small>(Mtoe 9,425)</small></div>\n</td>\n<td>23,816</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-IEA-Report-keyworld-2016_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-Report-keyworld-2016-9">[9]</a></sup></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2015</td>\n<td>\n<div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;line-height: 100%; padding: 4px 0 0;">168,519<br />\n<small>(Quad BTU 575)</small></div>\n</td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-International-Energy-Outlook-2017_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-International-Energy-Outlook-2017-10">[10]</a></sup></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="5" style="font-size: 0.85em; font-weight: normal; text-align: left; padding: 6px 2px 4px 4px;"><sup>1</sup> converted from <a href="/wiki/Tonne_of_oil_equivalent" title="Tonne of oil equivalent">Mtoe</a> into TWh (1&#160;Mtoe = 11.63&#160;TWh)<br />\nand from <a href="/wiki/Quad_(unit)" title="Quad (unit)">Quad BTU</a> into TWh (1&#160;Quad BTU = 293.07&#160;TWh)</th>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<p>World <a href="/wiki/Total_primary_energy_supply" class="mw-redirect" title="Total primary energy supply">total primary energy supply</a> (TPES), or "primary energy" differs from the world final energy consumption because much of the energy that is acquired by humans is lost as other forms of energy during the process of its refinement into usable forms of energy and its transport from its initial place of supply to consumers. For instance, when oil is extracted from the ground it must be refined into gasoline, so that it can be used in a car, and transported over long distances to gas stations where it can be used by consumers. World final energy consumption refers to the fraction of the world\'s primary energy that is used in its final form by humanity.</p>\n<p>In 2014, world primary energy supply amounted to 155,481&#160;terawatt-hour (TWh) or 13,541&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tonne_of_oil_equivalent" title="Tonne of oil equivalent">Mtoe</a>, while the world final energy consumption was 109,613&#160;TWh or about 29.5% less than the total supply.<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-Report-keyworld-2016_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-Report-keyworld-2016-9">[9]</a></sup> World final energy consumption includes products as lubricants, asphalt and petrochemicals which have chemical energy content but are not used as fuel. This non-energy use amounted to 9,404 TWh (809 Mtoe) in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>\n<p>By the end of 2014, the total installed global power generating capacity is nearly 6.142 <a href="/wiki/Terawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Terawatt">TW</a> (million MW) which does not include the <a href="/wiki/Diesel_generator" title="Diesel generator">DG</a> sets not connected to local <a href="/wiki/Electricity_grid" class="mw-redirect" title="Electricity grid">electricity grids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> In 2014, world energy consumption for electricity generation was coal 40.8%, natural gas 21.6%, nuclear 10.6%, hydro 16.4%, \'others\' (solar, wind, geothermal, biomass, etc.) 6.3% and oil 4.3%. Coal and natural gas were the most popular energy fuels for generating electricity. The world\'s electricity consumption was 18,608&#160;TWh<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> in 2012. This figure is about 18% smaller than the generated electricity, due to grid losses, storage losses, and self-consumption from power plants (<a href="/wiki/Gross_generation" title="Gross generation">gross generation</a>). <a href="/wiki/Cogeneration" title="Cogeneration">Cogeneration</a> (CHP) power stations use some of the energy that is otherwise wasted for heating buildings or in industrial processes.</p>\n<div class="thumb tright" style="">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:200px">\n<div style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black">\n<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:0; top:0; border-width:0 200px 200px 0; border-color:#00CC4B"></div>\n<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 239.2922834842px; border-left-color:#7C6250"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#7C6250"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#7C6250"></div>\n<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 127.26004669635px; border-left-color:#de2821"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#de2821"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#de2821"></div>\n<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 24.340394514935px; border-left-color:#005CE6"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#005CE6"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#005CE6"></div>\n<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:100px; border-width:70.264996979885px 71.153567720928px 0 0; border-top-color:#ef8e39"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#ef8e39"></div>\n<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:0; border-width:0 153.28270598844px 100px 0; border-right-color:#313c42"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;right:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 100px 0;border-color:#313c42"></div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<p>2014 World electricity generation (23,816 TWh) by source (IEA, 2016)<sup id="cite_ref-iea16_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea16-13">[13]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #313c42; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Coal/Peat (40.8%)</div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ef8e39; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Natural Gas (21.6%)</div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #005CE6; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Hydro (16.4%)</div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #de2821; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Nuclear (10.6%)</div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #7C6250; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Oil (4.3%)</div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00CC4B; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Others (<a href="/wiki/Renewables" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewables">Renew.</a>) (6.3%)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The United States <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a> regularly publishes a report on world consumption for most types of primary energy resources. For 2013, estimated world energy consumption was 5.67 \xc3\x97 10<sup>20</sup> joules, or 157,481&#160;TWh. According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">IEA</a> the total world energy consumption was 143,851 TWh in 2008, 133,602&#160;TWh in 2005, 117,687&#160;TWh in 2000, and 102,569&#160;TWh in 1990. Total world electricity consumption was 19,504&#160;TWh in 2013, 16,503&#160;TWh in 2008, 15,105&#160;TWh in 2005, and 12,116&#160;TWh in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-Report-keyworld-2015_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-Report-keyworld-2015-2">[2]</a></sup> In 2012 approximately 22% of world energy was consumed in North America, 5% was consumed South and Central America, 23% was consumed in Europe and Eurasia, 3% was consumed in Africa, and 40% was consumed in the Asia Pacific region.<sup id="cite_ref-BP-Review-2013_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP-Review-2013-1">[1]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Recently there has been a large increase in international agreements and national Energy Action Plans, such as the EU 2009 Renewable Energy Directive, to increase the use of renewable energy due to the growing concerns about pollution from energy sources that come from fossil fuels such as oil, coal, and natural gas.<sup id="cite_ref-undp_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-undp-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Global_energy:_perspectives_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Global_energy:_perspectives-14">[14]</a></sup> One such initiative was the United Nations Development Programme\'s World Energy Assessment in 2000 that highlighted many challenges humanity would have to overcome in order to shift from fossil fuels to renewable energy sources.<sup id="cite_ref-undp_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-undp-3">[3]</a></sup> From 2000\xe2\x80\x932012 renewable energy grew at a rate higher than any other point in history, with a consumption increase of 176.5 million tonnes of oil. During this period, oil, coal, and natural gas continued to grow and had increases that were much higher than the increase in renewable energy. The following figures illustrate the growth in consumption of fossil fuels such as oil, coal, and natural gas as well as renewable sources of energy during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-BP-Review-2013_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP-Review-2013-1">[1]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trends">Trends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Trends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_primary_energy_consumption_in_quadrillion_Btu_by_region.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/World_primary_energy_consumption_in_quadrillion_Btu_by_region.svg/220px-World_primary_energy_consumption_in_quadrillion_Btu_by_region.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/World_primary_energy_consumption_in_quadrillion_Btu_by_region.svg/330px-World_primary_energy_consumption_in_quadrillion_Btu_by_region.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/World_primary_energy_consumption_in_quadrillion_Btu_by_region.svg/440px-World_primary_energy_consumption_in_quadrillion_Btu_by_region.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="404" data-file-height="422" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_primary_energy_consumption_in_quadrillion_Btu_by_region.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nWorld primary energy consumption in <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(numbers)#1015" title="Orders of magnitude (numbers)">quadrillion</a> <a href="/wiki/Btu" class="mw-redirect" title="Btu">Btu</a><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Energy_intensity_comparison_(2004).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Energy_intensity_comparison_%282004%29.svg/220px-Energy_intensity_comparison_%282004%29.svg.png" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Energy_intensity_comparison_%282004%29.svg/330px-Energy_intensity_comparison_%282004%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Energy_intensity_comparison_%282004%29.svg/440px-Energy_intensity_comparison_%282004%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="860" data-file-height="540" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Energy_intensity_comparison_(2004).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<b>Energy intensity of different economies:</b> The graph shows the ratio between energy usage and GDP for selected countries. GDP is based on 2004 purchasing power parity and 2000 dollars adjusted for inflation.<sup id="cite_ref-EIAtablee1_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIAtablee1-16">[16]</a></sup></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Japan_energy_and_GDP.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Japan_energy_and_GDP.svg/220px-Japan_energy_and_GDP.svg.png" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Japan_energy_and_GDP.svg/330px-Japan_energy_and_GDP.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Japan_energy_and_GDP.svg/440px-Japan_energy_and_GDP.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="570" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Japan_energy_and_GDP.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<b>GDP and energy consumption in Japan, 1958\xe2\x80\x932000:</b> The data shows the correlation between GDP and energy use; however, it also shows that this link can be broken. After the oil shocks of 1973 and 1979 the energy use stagnated while Japan\'s GDP continued to grow, after 1985, under the influence of the then much cheaper oil, energy use resumed its historical relation to GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The energy consumption growth in the G20 slowed down to 2% in 2011, after the strong increase of 2010. The economic crisis is largely responsible for this slow growth. For several years now, the world energy demand is characterized by the bullish Chinese and Indian markets, while developed countries struggle with stagnant economies, high oil prices, resulting in stable or decreasing energy consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>\n<p>According to <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">IEA</a> data from 1990 to 2008, the average energy use per person increased 10% while world population increased 27%. Regional energy use also grew from 1990 to 2008: the Middle East increased by 170%, China by 146%, India by 91%, Africa by 70%, Latin America by 66%, the USA by 20%, the EU-27 block by 7%, and world overall grew by 39%.</p>\n<p>In 2008, total worldwide primary energy consumption was 132,000 terawatt-hours (<a href="/wiki/TWh" class="mw-redirect" title="TWh">TWh</a>) or 474 exajoules (<a href="/wiki/Exajoules" class="mw-redirect" title="Exajoules">EJ</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-EIA_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIA-19">[19]</a></sup> In 2012, primary energy demand increased to 158,000 TWh (567&#160;EJ).<sup id="cite_ref-EIAIES2015_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIAIES2015-20">[20]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Energy consumption in the <a href="/wiki/Group_of_Twenty_Finance_Ministers_and_Central_Bank_Governors" class="mw-redirect" title="Group of Twenty Finance Ministers and Central Bank Governors">G20</a> increased by more than 5% in 2010 after a slight decline of 2009. In 2009, world energy consumption decreased for the first time in 30 years by 1.1%, or about 130 million tonnes of oil equivalent (<a href="/wiki/Tonne_of_oil_equivalent" title="Tonne of oil equivalent">Mtoe</a>), as a result of the financial and economic crisis, which reduced world GDP by 0.6% in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>\n<p>This evolution is the result of two contrasting trends: Energy consumption growth remained vigorous in several developing countries, specifically in Asia (+4%). Conversely, in OECD, consumption was severely cut by 4.7% in 2009 and was thus almost down to its 2000 levels. In North America, Europe and the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Independent_States" title="Commonwealth of Independent States">CIS</a>, consumptions shrank by 4.5%, 5% and 8.5% respectively due to the slowdown in economic activity. China became the world\'s largest energy consumer (18% of the total) since its consumption surged by 8% during 2009 (up from 4% in 2008). Oil remained the largest energy source (33%) despite the fact that its share has been decreasing over time. Coal posted a growing role in the world\'s energy consumption: in 2009, it accounted for 27% of the total.</p>\n<p>Most energy is used in the country of origin, since it is cheaper to transport final products than raw materials. In 2008, the share export of the total energy production by fuel was: oil 50% (1,952/3,941&#160;Mt), gas 25% (800/3,149&#160;bcm) and hard coal 14% (793/5,845&#160;Mt).<sup id="cite_ref-IEA20109_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA20109-22">[22]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Most of the world\'s high energy resources are from the conversion of the sun\'s rays to other energy forms after being incident upon the planet. Some of that energy has been preserved as fossil energy, some is directly or indirectly usable; for example, via solar PV/thermal, wind, hydro- or wave power. The total solar irradiance is measured by satellite to be roughly 1361 watts per square meter <i>(see <a href="/wiki/Solar_constant" title="Solar constant">solar constant</a>)</i>, though it fluctuates by about 6.9% during the year due to the Earth\'s varying distance from the sun. This value, after multiplication by the cross-sectional area intercepted by the Earth, is the total rate of <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy#Energy_from_the_Sun" title="Solar energy">solar energy</a> received by the planet; about half, 89,000&#160;TW, reaches the Earth\'s surface.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The estimates of remaining non-renewable worldwide energy resources vary, with the remaining fossil fuels totaling an estimated 0.4&#160; yottajoule (<a href="/wiki/Yottajoule" class="mw-redirect" title="Yottajoule">YJ</a>) or 4 \xc3\x97 10<sup>23</sup> joules, and the available nuclear fuel such as <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> exceeding 2.5&#160;YJ. Fossil fuels range from 0.6 to 3&#160;YJ if estimates of reserves of <a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrates" class="mw-redirect" title="Methane clathrates">methane clathrates</a> are accurate and become technically extractable. The total power flux from the sun intercepting the Earth is 5.5&#160;YJ per year, though not all of this is available for human consumption. The IEA estimates for the world to meet global energy demand for the two decades from 2015 to 2035 it will require investment of $48 trillion and "credible policy frameworks."<sup id="cite_ref-IEAreport_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEAreport-24">[24]</a></sup></p>\n<p>According to IEA (2012) the goal of limiting warming to 2&#160;\xc2\xb0C is becoming more difficult and costly with each year that passes. If action is not taken before 2017, CO<sub>2</sub> emissions would be locked-in by energy infrastructure existing in 2017. Fossil fuels are dominant in the global <a href="/wiki/Energy_mix" title="Energy mix">energy mix</a>, supported by $523 billion subsidies in 2011, up almost 30% on 2010 and six times more than subsidies to renewables.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>\n<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders" style="text-align:right;">\n<caption>Regional energy use (kWh/capita &amp; TWh) and growth 1990\xe2\x80\x932008 (%)<sup id="cite_ref-energil.C3.A4get2010_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energil.C3.A4get2010-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IEA2010Oil_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA2010Oil-27">[27]</a></sup></caption>\n<tr>\n<th></th>\n<th scope="col" colspan="3">kWh/capita</th>\n<th scope="col" Union">EU-28</a></th>\n<td>40,240</td>\n<td>40,821</td>\n<td>1%</td>\n<td>473</td>\n<td>499</td>\n<td>5%</td>\n<td>19.0</td>\n<td>20.4</td>\n<td>7%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Middle style="text-align:left;font-size:smaller;">\n<td colspan="10">Source: IEA/OECD, Population OECD/World Bank\n<ul>\n<li>Energy use = kWh/capita* Mrd. capita (population) = 1000&#160;TWh</li>\n<li>Others: Mathematically calculated, includes e.g. countries in Asia and Australia. The use of energy varies between the "other countries": E.g. in Australia, Japan, or Canada energy is used more per capita than in Bangladesh or Burma.</li>\n</ul>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emissions">Emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a> <a href="/wiki/Exhaust_gas" title="Exhaust gas">emissions</a> resulting from energy production are an <a href="/wiki/Environmental_problem" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental problem">environmental problem</a>. Efforts to resolve this include the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a>, which is a UN agreement aiming to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">reduce harmful climate impacts</a>, which a number of nations have signed. Limiting global temperature increase to 2 degrees Celsius, thought to be a risk by the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_Environmental_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Stockholm Environmental Institute">SEI</a>, is now doubtful.</p>\n<p>To limit global temperature to a hypothetical 2 degrees Celsius rise would demand a 75% decline in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon emissions">carbon emissions</a> in industrial countries by 2050, if the population is 10 billion in 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Across 40 years, this averages to a 2% decrease every year. In 2011, the emissions of energy production continued rising regardless of the consensus of the basic problem. Hypothetically, according to <a href="/wiki/Robert_Engelman" title="Robert Engelman">Robert Engelman</a> (Worldwatch institute), in order to prevent collapse, human civilization would have to stop increasing emissions within a decade regardless of the economy or population (2009).<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gases" class="mw-redirect" title="Greenhouse gases">Greenhouse gases</a> are not the only emissions of energy production and consumption. Large amounts of pollutants such as <a href="/wiki/Sulphurous_oxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Sulphurous oxide">sulphurous oxides</a> (SO<sub>x</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxides</a> (NO<sub>x</sub>), and <a href="/wiki/Particulate_matter" class="mw-redirect" title="Particulate matter">particulate matter</a> (PM) are produced from the combustion of fossil fuels and biomass; the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="World Health Organisation">World Health Organisation</a> estimates that 7 million premature deaths are caused each year by <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-who.int_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">Biomass</a> <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> is a major contributor.<sup id="cite_ref-who.int_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In addition to producing air pollution like <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> combustion, most biomass has high CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="By_source">By source</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: By source">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright" style="">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:200px">\n<div style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black">\n<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:0; top:0; border-width:0 200px 200px 0; border-color:#de2821"></div>\n<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 321.45820786341px; border-left-color:#00CC4B"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#00CC4B"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#00CC4B"></div>\n<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:0; border-width:0 41.789897502735px 100px 0; border-right-color:#7C6250"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;right:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 100px 0;border-color:#7C6250"></div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<p>Total primary energy supply of 13,699 <a href="/wiki/Mega-" title="Mega-">mega-</a><a href="/wiki/Tonne_of_oil_equivalent" title="Tonne of oil equivalent">toe</a> by source in 2014 (IEA, 2016)<sup id="cite_ref-iea16_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea16-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IEA-Report-keyworld-2014_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-Report-keyworld-2014-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:28</sup></p>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #de2821; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Nuclear (4.8%)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_fuels">Fossil fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Fossil fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">Fossil fuel</a></div>\n<p>The twentieth century saw a rapid twenty-fold increase in the use of fossil fuels. Between 1980 and 2006, the worldwide annual growth rate was 2%.<sup id="cite_ref-EIA_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIA-19">[19]</a></sup> According to the US <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a>\'s 2006 estimate, the estimated 471.8&#160;EJ total consumption in 2004, was divided as given in the table above, with fossil fuels supplying 86% of the world\'s energy:</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal">Coal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Coal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">Coal</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_coal_production" title="List of countries by coal production">List of countries by coal production</a></div>\n<p>In 2000, China accounted for 28% of world coal consumption, other Asia consumed 19%, North America 25% and the EU 14%. The single greatest coal-consuming country is China. Its share of the world coal production was 28% in 2000 and rose to 48% in 2009. In contrast to China\'s ~70% increase in coal consumption, world coal use increased 48% from 2000 to 2009. In practice, the majority of this growth occurred in China and the rest in other Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-energisve2010Coal_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energisve2010Coal-34">[34]</a></sup> China\'s energy consumption is mostly driven by the industry sector, the majority of which comes from coal consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>\n<p>World annual coal production increased 1,905&#160;Mt or 32% in 6 years in 2011 compared to 2005, of which over 70% was in China and 8% in India. Coal production was in 2011 7,783&#160;Mt, and 2009 6,903&#160;Mt, equal to 12.7% production increase in two years.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>\n<p>If production and consumption of coal continue at the rate as in 2008, proven and economically recoverable world reserves of coal would last for about 150 years. This is much more than needed for an irreversible climate catastrophe. Coal is the largest source of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> emissions in the world. According to <a href="/wiki/James_Hansen" title="James Hansen">James Hansen</a> the single most important action needed to tackle the climate crisis is to reduce CO<sub>2</sub> emissions from <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">coal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Indonesia" title="Energy in Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Australia" title="Energy in Australia">Australia</a> exported together 57.1% of the world coal export in 2011. China, Japan, South Korea, India and Taiwan had 65% share of all the world coal import in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>\n<table class="wikitable floatleft" style="text-align: right; margin-left: 22px;">\n<caption><span class="nowrap">Regional coal supply (TWh), share 2010 (%) and share of change 2000\xe2\x80\x932010<sup id="cite_ref-energisve2010Coal_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energisve2010Coal-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Energil.C3.A4get_2011_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Energil.C3.A4get_2011-39">[39]</a></sup></span></caption>\n<tr>\n<th scope="col">Region</th>\n<th scope="col">2000</th>\n<th scope="col">2008</th>\n<th scope="col">2009*</th>\n<th scope="col">2010*</th>\n<th scope="col">&#160;%*</th>\n<th scope="col">Change<br />\n2000\xe2\x80\x932009*</th>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">North America</th>\n<td>6,654</td>\n<td>6,740</td>\n<td>6,375</td>\n<td>6,470</td>\n<td>16%</td>\n<td>\xe2\x88\x921.2%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Asia excl. style="clear:left;"></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil">Oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Oil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">Petroleum</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_oil_production" title="List of countries by oil production">List of countries by oil production</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_oil_consumption" title="List of countries by oil consumption">List of countries by oil consumption</a></div>\n<p>Coal fueled the industrial revolution in the 18th and 19th century. With the advent of the automobile, aeroplanes and the spreading use of electricity, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a> became the dominant fuel during the twentieth century. The growth of oil as the largest fossil fuel was further enabled by steadily dropping prices from 1920 until 1973. After the oil shocks of <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973</a> and <a href="/wiki/1979_energy_crisis" title="1979 energy crisis">1979</a>, during which the price of oil increased from 5 to 45 US dollars per barrel, there was a shift away from oil.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Coal, natural gas, and nuclear became the fuels of choice for electricity generation and conservation measures increased energy efficiency. In the U.S. the average car more than doubled the number of miles per gallon. Japan, which bore the brunt of the oil shocks, made spectacular improvements and now has the highest energy efficiency in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-IEAKey_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEAKey-42">[42]</a></sup> From 1965 to 2008, the use of fossil fuels has continued to grow and their share of the energy supply has increased. From 2003 to 2008, coal was the fastest growing fossil fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>\n<p>It is estimated that between 100 and 135 billion tonnes of oil has been consumed between 1850 and the present.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_Gas">Natural Gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Natural Gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Natural_Gas" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural Gas">Natural Gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_production" title="List of countries by natural gas production">List of countries by natural gas production</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_consumption" title="List of countries by natural gas consumption">List of countries by natural gas consumption</a></div>\n<p>In 2009, the world use of natural gas grew 31% compared to 2000. 66% of this growth was outside EU, North America, Latin America, and Russia. Others include the Middle East, Asia, and Africa. The gas supply increased also in the previous regions: 8.6% in the EU and 16% in the North America 2000\xe2\x80\x932009.<sup id="cite_ref-energil.C3.A4get2010G_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energil.C3.A4get2010G-45">[45]</a></sup></p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: right; margin-left: 22px;">\n<caption>Regional gas supply (TWh) and share 2010 (%)<sup id="cite_ref-Energil.C3.A4get_2011_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Energil.C3.A4get_2011-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-energil.C3.A4get2010G_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energil.C3.A4get2010G-45">[45]</a></sup></caption>\n<tr>\n<th>Land</th>\n<th>2000</th>\n<th>2008</th>\n<th>2009</th>\n<th>2010</th>\n<th>&#160;%</th>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">North America</td>\n<td>7,621</td>\n<td>7,779</td>\n<td>8,839</td>\n<td>8,925</td>\n<td>27%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">Asia excl. font-size: 0.85em; padding: 6px 3px;">Source: IEA, in 2009, 2010 BP</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_power">Nuclear power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nuclear power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Power" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear Power">Nuclear Power</a></div>\n<p>As of 1 July 2016, the world had 444 operable grid-electric nuclear power reactors with 62 others under construction.<sup id="cite_ref-WNA2016_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WNA2016-46">[46]</a></sup> Since commercial nuclear energy began in the mid 1950s, 2008 was the first year that no new nuclear power plant was connected to the grid, although two were connected in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-tf2010_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tf2010-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Annual generation of nuclear power has been on a slight downward trend since 2007, decreasing 1.8% in 2009 to 2558&#160;TWh, and another 1.6% in 2011 to 2518&#160;TWh, despite increases in production from most countries worldwide, because those increases were more than offset by decreases in Germany and Japan. Nuclear power met 11.7% of the world\'s electricity demand in 2011. Source: IEA/OECD<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-Report-2013_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-Report-2013-6">[6]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewable_energy">Renewable energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Renewable energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_renewable_energy_topics_by_country" title="List of renewable energy topics by country">List of renewable energy topics by country</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialization" title="Renewable energy commercialization">Renewable energy commercialization</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tmulti tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_(Ipsos_2011).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png/220px-Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png" width="220" height="136" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png/330px-Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png/440px-Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png 2x" data-file-width="1030" data-file-height="637" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Strong public support for <a href="/wiki/Renewables" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewables">renewables</a> worldwide in 2011<sup id="cite_ref-ipsos_2011_preference_for_renewables_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipsos_2011_preference_for_renewables-49">[49]</a></sup></div>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:left"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Renewable energy is generally defined as energy that comes from <a href="/wiki/Renewable_resource" title="Renewable resource">resources</a> that are not significantly depleted by their use, such as <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wave_power" title="Wave power">waves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal heat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Renewable energy is gradually replacing conventional fuels in four distinct areas: <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_hot_water" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar hot water">hot water</a>/<a href="/wiki/Space_heating" class="mw-redirect" title="Space heating">space heating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Motor_fuel" title="Motor fuel">motor fuels</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stand-alone_power_system" title="Stand-alone power system">rural (off-grid)</a> energy services.<sup id="cite_ref-ren15_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ren15-51">[51]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Based on <a href="/wiki/REN21" title="REN21">REN21</a>\'s 2014 report, renewables contributed 19 percent to our energy consumption and 22 percent to our electricity generation in 2012 and 2013, respectively. This energy consumption is divided as 9% coming from traditional biomass, 4.2% as heat energy (non-biomass), 3.8% hydro electricity and 2% electricity from wind, solar, geothermal, and biomass. Worldwide investments in renewable technologies amounted to more than US$214 billion in 2013, with countries like <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_China" title="Renewable energy in China">China</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_the_United_States" title="Renewable energy in the United States">United States</a> heavily investing in wind, hydro, solar and biofuels.<sup id="cite_ref-ria21-GSR2014_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ria21-GSR2014-52">[52]</a></sup> Renewable energy resources exist over wide geographical areas, in contrast to other energy sources, which are concentrated in a limited number of countries. Rapid deployment of renewable energy and <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficiency</a> is resulting in significant <a href="/wiki/Energy_security_and_renewable_technology" title="Energy security and renewable technology">energy security</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">climate change mitigation</a>, and economic benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-International_Energy_Agency_2012_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-International_Energy_Agency_2012-53">[53]</a></sup> In international public opinion surveys there is strong support for promoting renewable sources such as solar power and wind power.<sup id="cite_ref-UNEP_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP-54">[54]</a></sup> At the national level, at least 30 nations around the world already have renewable energy contributing more than 20 percent of energy supply. National renewable energy markets are projected to continue to grow strongly in the coming decade and beyond.<sup id="cite_ref-REN21_2013_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-REN21_2013-55">[55]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The following table shows increasing <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a>, and has <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factors</a> that range from 11% for solar, to 40% for hydropower.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a energy use 40,500 TWh.</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydro">Hydro</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Hydro">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectricity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">Hydropower</a></div>\n<p>Hydroelectricity is the term referring to electricity generated by <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a>; the production of electrical power through the use of the kinetic energy of falling or flowing water. In 2015 hydropower generated 16.6% of the world\'s total electricity and 70% of all renewable electricity,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> which continues the rapid rate of increase experienced between 2003 and 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-wi2012_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wi2012-65">[65]</a></sup> Hydropower is produced in 150 countries, with the Asia-Pacific region generating 32 percent of global hydropower in 2010. China is the largest hydroelectricity producer, with 2,600&#160;PJ (721&#160;TWh) of production in 2010, representing around 17% of domestic electricity use. There are now three hydroelectricity plants larger than 10&#160;GW: the <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> in China, <a href="/wiki/Itaipu_Dam" title="Itaipu Dam">Itaipu Dam</a> in Brazil, and <a href="/wiki/Guri_Dam" title="Guri Dam">Guri Dam</a> in Venezuela.<sup id="cite_ref-wi2012_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wi2012-65">[65]</a></sup> Nine of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_electricity_production_from_renewable_sources" title="List of countries by electricity production from renewable sources">worlds top 10 renewable electricity producers</a> are primarily hydroelectric, one is wind.</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Marine_energy">Marine energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Marine energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ocean_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Ocean energy">Ocean energy</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Marine_energy" title="Marine energy">Marine energy</a>, also known as <i>ocean energy</i> and <i>marine and hydrokinetic energy</i> (MHK) includes <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tidal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wave_power" title="Wave power">wave</a> power and is a relatively new sector of renewable energy, with most projects still in the pilot phase, but the theoretical potential is equivalent to 4\xe2\x80\x9318 million tonne of oil equivalent (toe). MHK development in U.S. and international waters includes projects using devices such as, wave energy converters in open coastal areas with significant waves, tidal turbines placed in coastal and estuarine areas, in-stream <a href="/wiki/Turbine" title="Turbine">turbines</a> in fast-moving <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a>, ocean current turbines in areas of strong <a href="/wiki/Marine_current" class="mw-redirect" title="Marine current">marine currents</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ocean_thermal_energy_converter" class="mw-redirect" title="Ocean thermal energy converter">ocean thermal energy converters</a> in deep tropical waters.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind">Wind</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Wind">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">Wind power</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_by_country" title="Wind power by country">Wind power by country</a></div>\n<p>Wind power is growing at the rate of 17% annually, with a worldwide <a href="/wiki/Installed_wind_power_capacity" class="mw-redirect" title="Installed wind power capacity">installed capacity</a> of 432,883&#160;megawatts (MW) at the end of 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-greeninc.blogs.nytimes.com_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greeninc.blogs.nytimes.com-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-REN21_2011_15_2_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-REN21_2011_15_2-69">[69]</a></sup> and is widely used in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_European_Union" title="Wind power in the European Union">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_China" title="Wind power in China">Asia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_States" title="Wind power in the United States">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Glob_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glob-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Several countries have achieved relatively high levels of wind power penetration, such as 21% of stationary electricity production in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Denmark" title="Wind power in Denmark">Denmark</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-wwea_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wwea-72">[72]</a></sup> 18% in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Portugal" title="Wind power in Portugal">Portugal</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-wwea_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wwea-72">[72]</a></sup> 16% in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Spain" title="Wind power in Spain">Spain</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-wwea_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wwea-72">[72]</a></sup> 14% in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind power in Ireland">Ireland</a><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> and 9% in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Germany" title="Wind power in Germany">Germany</a> in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-wwea_72-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wwea-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ren212011_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ren212011-74">[74]</a></sup> As of 2011, 83 countries around the world are using wind power on a commercial basis.<sup id="cite_ref-ren212011_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ren212011-74">[74]</a></sup> Continuing strong growth, by 2016 wind generated 3% of global power annually.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar">Solar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Solar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">Solar energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">Solar power</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_by_country" title="Solar power by country">Solar power by country</a></div>\n<p>Solar energy, radiant <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a>, has been harnessed by humans since <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">ancient times</a> using a range of ever-evolving technologies. Solar energy technologies include <a href="/wiki/Solar_heating" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar heating">solar heating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_photovoltaics" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar photovoltaics">solar photovoltaics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">concentrated solar power</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_architecture" title="Solar architecture">solar architecture</a>, which can make considerable contributions to solving some of the most urgent problems the world now faces. The <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> projected that solar power could provide "a third of the global final energy demand after 2060, while CO<sub>2</sub> emissions would be reduced to very low levels."<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Solar technologies are broadly characterized as either <a href="/wiki/Passive_solar" class="mw-redirect" title="Passive solar">passive solar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Active_solar" title="Active solar">active solar</a> depending on the way they capture, convert and distribute solar energy. Active solar techniques include the use of <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_system" title="Photovoltaic system">photovoltaic systems</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_collector" title="Solar thermal collector">solar thermal collectors</a> to harness the energy. Passive solar techniques include orienting a building to the Sun, selecting materials with favorable <a href="/wiki/Thermal_mass" title="Thermal mass">thermal mass</a> or light dispersing properties, and designing spaces that <a href="/wiki/Ventilation_(architecture)" title="Ventilation (architecture)">naturally circulate air</a>. From 2012 to 2016 solar capacity tripled and now provides 1.3% of global energy.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Geothermal">Geothermal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Geothermal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">Geothermal energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">Geothermal power</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">Geothermal energy</a> is used commercially in over 70 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-INEL_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INEL-78">[78]</a></sup> In 2004, 200 petajoules (56&#160;TWh) of electricity was generated from geothermal resources, and an additional 270 petajoules (75&#160;TWh) of geothermal energy was used directly, mostly for space heating. In 2007, the world had a global capacity for <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7010100000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>10&#160;GW</span> of electricity generation and an additional <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7010280000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>28&#160;GW</span> of <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_heating" title="Geothermal heating">direct heating</a>, including extraction by <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_heat_pumps" class="mw-redirect" title="Geothermal heat pumps">geothermal heat pumps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Renewables2006_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Renewables2006-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GEO_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GEO-80">[80]</a></sup> Heat pumps are small and widely distributed, so estimates of their total capacity are uncertain and range up to <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7011100000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>100&#160;GW</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-INEL_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INEL-78">[78]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bio_energy">Bio energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Bio energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">Biomass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">Biogas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">Biofuel</a></div>\n<p>Until the beginning of the nineteenth century biomass was the predominant fuel, today it has only a small share of the overall energy supply. Electricity produced from <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> sources was estimated at 44&#160;GW for 2005. Biomass electricity generation increased by over 100% in Germany, Hungary, the Netherlands, Poland, and Spain. A further 220&#160;GW was used for heating (in 2004), bringing the total energy consumed from biomass to around 264&#160;GW. The use of biomass fires for cooking is excluded.<sup id="cite_ref-Renewables2006_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Renewables2006-79">[79]</a></sup> World production of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">bioethanol</a> increased by 8% in 2005 to reach 33 <a href="/wiki/Gigalitre" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigalitre">gigalitres</a> (8.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/US_gallon" class="mw-redirect" title="US gallon">US&#160;gal</a>), with most of the increase in the United States, bringing it level to the levels of consumption in Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-Renewables2006_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Renewables2006-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</a> increased by 85% to 3.9 gigalitres (1.0<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;US&#160;gal), making it the fastest growing renewable energy source in 2005. Over 50% is produced in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-Renewables2006_79-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Renewables2006-79">[79]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="By_country">By country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: By country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_renewable_energy_topics_by_country" title="List of renewable energy topics by country">List of renewable energy topics by country</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Energy_by_country" title="Category:Energy by country">Energy by country</a>, <a href="/wiki/Worldwide_energy_supply" title="Worldwide energy supply">Worldwide energy supply</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_energy_consumption_per_capita" title="List of countries by energy consumption per capita">List of countries by energy consumption per capita</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright" style="">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:200px">\n<div style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black">\n<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:0; top:0; border-width:0 239.2922834842px 100px 0; border-right-color:#FFFF57"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;right:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 100px 0;border-color:#FFFF57"></div>\n<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:0; border-width:0 112.00117924292px 100px 0; border-right-color:#66CCFF"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;right:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 100px 0;border-color:#66CCFF"></div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<p>World total final consumption of 13,699 <a href="/wiki/Tonne_of_oil_equivalent" title="Tonne of oil equivalent">Mtoe</a> by region in 2014 (IEA, 2016)<sup id="cite_ref-iea16_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea16-13">[13]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #66CCFF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">OECD</a> (38.4%)</div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFFF57; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Middle East (5.3%)</div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #009a39; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Non-OECD Eurasia (8.2%)</div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #de2821; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;China (22.4%)</div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:pink; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Rest of Asia (12.7%)</div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #211873; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Non-OECD Americas (4.7%)</div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ef8e39; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Africa (5.6%)</div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #313c42; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Bunkers (2.7%)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Energy consumption is loosely correlated with <a href="/wiki/Gross_National_Product#Gross_National_Product" class="mw-redirect" title="Gross National Product">gross national product</a> and climate, but there is a large difference even between the most highly developed countries, such as Japan and Germany with an energy consumption rate of 6&#160;kW per person and the <a href="/wiki/Energy_use_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Energy use in the United States">United States</a> with an energy consumption rate of 11.4&#160;kW per person. In developing countries, particularly those that are sub-tropical or tropical such as India, the per person energy use rate is closer to 0.7&#160;kW. Bangladesh has the lowest consumption rate with 0.2&#160;kW per person.</p>\n<p>The US consumes 25% of the world\'s energy with a share of global GDP at 22% and a share of the world population at 4.59%.<sup id="cite_ref-World_Population_Prospects_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Population_Prospects-81">[81]</a></sup> The most significant growth of energy consumption is currently taking place in China, which has been growing at 5.5% per year over the last 25 years. Its population of 1.3 billion people (19.6% of the world population<sup id="cite_ref-World_Population_Prospects_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Population_Prospects-81">[81]</a></sup>) is consuming energy at a rate of 1.6&#160;kW per person.</p>\n<p>One measurement of efficiency is <a href="/wiki/Energy_intensity" title="Energy intensity">energy intensity</a>. This is a measure of the amount of energy it takes a country to produce a dollar of gross domestic product.</p>\n<div class="thumb tmulti tnone center">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:513px;max-width:513px">\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:257px;max-width:257px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Energy_Use_per_Capita.png" class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The World Bank: PPP $ per kg of oil equivalent (2011)</div>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:left"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:left;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_2">Oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Petroleum">Petroleum</a></div>\n<p>Saudi Arabia, Russia and the United States accounted for 34% of oil production in 2011. Saudi Arabia, Russia and Nigeria accounted for 36% of oil export in 2011.</p>\n<table class="wikitable floatleft" style="text-align:right;">\n<caption>Top 10 <a href="/wiki/Oil" title="Oil">oil</a> class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: By sector">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders floatright" style="text-align:right;">\n<caption>World energy use by sector, 2012<sup id="cite_ref-EIA2016_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIA2016-86">[86]</a></sup></caption>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Sector</th>\n<th scope="col">10<sup>15</sup><a href="/wiki/Btu" class="mw-redirect" title="Btu">Btu</a></th>\n<th scope="col"><a href="/wiki/Petawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Petawatt">Petawatt</a>-hours</th>\n<th scope="col">percentage</th>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Residential</th>\n<td>53.0</td>\n<td>15.5</td>\n<td>13</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Commercial</th>\n<td>29.3</td>\n<td>8.6</td>\n<td>7</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Industrial</th>\n<td>222.3</td>\n<td>65.1</td>\n<td>54</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Transportation</th>\n<td>104.2</td>\n<td>30.5</td>\n<td>26</td>\n</tr>\n<tr style="font-weight:bold;">\n<th scope="row" style="font-weight:bold;">Total*</th>\n<td>408.9</td>\n<td>119.8</td>\n<td>100</td>\n</tr>\n<tr style="text-align:left;font-size:smaller;">\n<td colspan="5">Source: US DOE. PWh from 0.293 times Btu column.<br />\nNumbers are the end use of energy</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<p>The table to the right shows the amounts of energy consumed worldwide in 2012 by four sectors, according to the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a> of the <a href="/wiki/US_Department_of_Energy" class="mw-redirect" title="US Department of Energy">US Department of Energy</a>:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Residential (heating, lighting, and appliances)</li>\n<li>Commercial (lighting, heating and cooling of commercial buildings, and provision of water and sewer services)</li>\n<li>Industrial users (agriculture, mining, manufacturing, and construction)</li>\n<li>Transportation (passenger, freight, and pipeline)</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Of the total 120 PWh (<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7020432000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>120<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>15</sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Watt-hour" class="mw-redirect" title="Watt-hour">Wh</a></span>) consumed, 19.4 were in the form of electricity, but this electricity required 61.7 PWh to produce. Thus the total energy consumption was around 160 PWh (ca <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7020580280718941000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>550<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>15</sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/British_thermal_unit" title="British thermal unit">Btu</a></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-EIA2016_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIA2016-86">[86]</a></sup> The efficiency of a typical existing power plant is around 38%.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The new generation of gas-fired plants reaches a substantially higher efficiency of 55%. Coal is the most common fuel for the world\'s electricity plants.<sup id="cite_ref-coal_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coal-88">[88]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Another report gives different values for the sectors, apparently due to different definitions. According to this, total world energy use per sector in 2008 was industry 28%, transport 27% and residential and service 36%. Division was about the same in the year 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-energisve2010xS_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energisve2010xS-89">[89]</a></sup></p>\n<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders floatleft" style="text-align:right;">\n<caption>World energy use per sector<sup id="cite_ref-energisve2010xS_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energisve2010xS-89">[89]</a></sup></caption>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Year</th>\n<th scope="col">2000</th>\n<th scope="col">2008</th>\n<th scope="col">2000</th>\n<th (2008) 143,851&#160;TWh</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: European Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Environmental_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="European Environmental Agency">European Environmental Agency</a> (EEA) measures final energy consumption (does not include energy used in production and lost in transportation) and finds that the transport sector is responsible for 31.8% of final energy consumption, households 26.2%, industry 25.6%, <a href="/wiki/Service_(economics)" title="Service (economics)">services</a> 13.5% and agriculture 2.9% in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The use of energy is responsible for the majority of greenhouse gas emissions (79%), with the energy sector representing 31%, transport 19%, industry 13%, households 9% and others 7%.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>\n<p>While <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">efficient energy use</a> and resource efficiency are growing as public policy issues, more than 70% of <a href="/wiki/Coal_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal plants">coal plants</a> in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> are more than 20 years old and operate at an efficiency level of between 32\xe2\x80\x9340%.<sup id="cite_ref-EC1_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EC1-92">[92]</a></sup> Technological developments in the 1990s have allowed efficiencies in the range of 40\xe2\x80\x9345% at newer plants.<sup id="cite_ref-EC1_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EC1-92">[92]</a></sup> However, according to an impact assessment by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>, this is still below the <a href="/wiki/Best_available_technology" title="Best available technology">best available technological</a> (BAT) efficiency levels of 46\xe2\x80\x9349%.<sup id="cite_ref-EC1_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EC1-92">[92]</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/Gas-fired_power_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas-fired power plant">gas-fired power plants</a> the average efficiency is 52% compared to 58\xe2\x80\x9359% with best available technology (BAT), and gas and oil <a href="/wiki/Boiler_(steam_generator)" class="mw-redirect" title="Boiler (steam generator)">boiler plants</a> operate at average 36% efficiency (BAT delivers 47%).<sup id="cite_ref-EC1_92-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EC1-92">[92]</a></sup> According to that same impact assessment by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>, raising the efficiency of all new plants and the majority of existing plants, through the setting of authorisation and permit conditions, to an average generation efficiency of 51.5% in 2020 would lead to a reduction in annual consumption of 15&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (3.6&#160;cu&#160;mi) of natural gas and 25&#160;Mt (25,000,000 long tons; 28,000,000 short tons) of coal.<sup id="cite_ref-EC1_92-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EC1-92">[92]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=World_energy_consumption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div
